[1mูุฑุงุกุงุช ุฅุถุงููุฉ [0m

The literature on the free will problem is enormous, and there is no possibility of providing anything like a comprehensive guide 
to it here. I have simply picked out a small number of representative works. 

[1mุงูููุฑุฉ ุงูุนุงูุฉ [0m

A useful collection of articles is [3mFree Will [0m, edited by Gary Watson (Oxford University Press, 1982; 2nd edn., 2003) in the Oxford 
Readings in Philosophy series. 

[3mThe Oxford Handbook of Free Will, [0medited by Robert Kane (Oxford University Press, 2002) contains articles on every area of the 
contemporary debate. 

For further reading on past theories of action from Plato and Aristotle onwards see Thomas Pink and Martin Stone (eds.), [3mThe Will 
and Human Action: From Antiquity to the Present Day [0m(Routledge, 2003). 

[1mุงูููุณูุฉ ุงููุฏููุฉ [0m

Aristotleโs [3mNicomachean Ethics [0mcontains a notable ancient discussion of action and moral responsibility. There are many modern 
English editions. Interesting modern discussions of Aristotle include [3mNecessity, Cause and Blame [0mby Richard Sorabji (Duckworth, 
1980) and [3mEthics with Aristotle [0mby Sarah Broadie (Oxford University Press, 1991). 

Much of later Greek thought now survives in somewhat fragmentary form. A very useful collection with excerpts from ancient texts 
and some critical discussion is [3mThe Hellenistic Philosophers [0m, edited by A. A. Long and D.N. Sedley (Cambridge University Press, 
1987โin two volumes, the first containing translations, the second containing original Greek texts). The collection covers 
problems to do with free will as well as many other areas of philosophy. 

Along with Aristotleโs [3mEthics [0m, Stoic theories of action exercised a profound influence on medieval thought. They are discussed in 
Brad Inwoodโs [3mEthics and Human Action in Early Stoicism [0m(Oxford University Press, 1985). A challenging but very interesting recent 
discussion of Stoic views of moral responsibility and freedom is [3mDeterminism and Freedom in Stoic Philosophy [0mby Suzanne Bobzien 
(Oxford University Press, 1998). 

One fundamental figure in late antiquity is St Augustine. His writings on freedom and the will are extensive, but their precise 
interpretation much disputed. A central text is [3mDe Libero Arbitrio [0m(On Free Choice). This can be found in a recent English 
translation by Thomas Williams (Hackett, 1993). 

[1mููุณูุฉ ุงููุฑูู ุงููุณุทู ูุนุตุฑ ุงูููุถุฉ [0m

A central figure in the 13th century is Thomas Aquinas. One very important discussion by him of action and freedom is to be found 
in the [3mSumma Theologiae [0m, his overview of theology and of related areas in philosophy. This extensive work is divided into three 
parts, and the second part deals with humans as rational beings. This second part is further subdivided into two. The first of 
these, the [3mPrima Secundae [0m, contains in questions 6โ17 an immensely interesting and detailed discussion of human actionโa 
discussion that has been the object of much study and commentary ever since. This discussion can be read in a useful multivolume 
dual Latin and English text edition prepared in the 1960s by the Dominican Fathers (Aquinasโs own teaching order). The relevant 
volume is 17, [3mThe Psychology of Human Acts [0medited by Thomas Gilby (Eyre & Spottiswoode, 1964). 

Modern discussions of Aquinas on action include Ralph McInernyโs [3mAquinas on Action [0m(Catholic University of America Press, 1992), 
and [3mRight Practical Reason [0mby Daniel Westberg (Oxford University Press, 1994). 

A key thinker of the 14th century is John Duns Scotus. A useful collection of his writings on the will and action, with critical 
discussion, is [3mDuns Scotus on the Will and Morality [0mby Allan Wolter (Catholic University of America Press, 1986). 

For a detailed discussion of medieval theories and a comparison of them with Hobbes see my โSuarez, Hobbes, and the Scholastic 
Tradition in Action Theoryโ, in Pink and Stone (eds.), [3mThe Will and Human Action. [0m

For Calvinโs [3mInstitutes of the Christian Religion [0mI have used the edition by McNeill and Battles in the Library of Christian 
Classics (Westminster Press, 1960). Those interested in Reformation disputes should also read the controversy between Luther and 
Erasmus, available under the title [3mLuther and Erasmus: Free Will and Salvation [0medited by E. Gordon Rupp and P. S. Watson (SCM 
Press, 1969). 

[1mููุจุฒุ ูููููุ ููุงูุท [0m

Central to understanding Hobbes on free will is his debate with Bishop Bramhall, published in London in 1656 as [3mThe Questions 
Concerning Liberty, Necessity and Chance [0m. Bramhall was the Anglican bishop of Derry, and shared an exile with Hobbes in Paris 
during the Civil Wars. In this debate Bramhall represented the will-based medieval scholastic tradition, and gave an account of 
human action and its freedom that owed much to thinkers such as Aquinas and Scotus. Hobbesโs criticism was acerbic and deeply 
influential. I am working on a modern edition of [3mThe Questions [0mfor the new Clarendon edition of the works of Hobbes. Part of 
Hobbesโs contribution to the debate exists separately under the title [3mOf Liberty and Necessity [0m. Excerpts from this work and from 
other of Hobbesโs writings are to be found in [3mBritish Moralists 1650โ1800 [0m, edited by D. D. Raphael (Hackett, 1991). Worth reading 
is the discussion of action and the passions at the beginning of Hobbesโs great political work [3mLeviathan [0m(see the beginning 
chapters, and especially chapter 6), edited by R. Tuck (Cambridge University Press, 1996). See also my paper on Hobbes and the 
medieval tradition mentioned above. 

An account of action and freedom that is more complex than Hobbesโs, but which clearly owes more than it admits to him, is to be 
found in book 2, chapter 21, โOf powerโ in John Lockeโs [3mEssay concerning Human Understanding [0mโsee the edition by P. H. Nidditch 
(Oxford University Press, 1975). 

Modern English-language Compatibilism owes much to David Hume. An important statement of his views is to be found in [3mAn Enquiry 
Concerning Human Understanding [0m, section 8, โOf Liberty and Necessityโโsee the edition by L. A. Selby-Bigge (Oxford University 
Press, 1975). Humeโs scepticism regarding our knowledge and experience of causation is stated in the preceding section 7 of the [3m
Enquiry [0mentitled โOf the Idea of Necessary Connexionโ. The interpretation of Hume on causation is disputedโsee [3mThe Sceptical 
Realism of David Hume [0mby John P. Wright (Manchester University Press, 1983) and Galen Strawsonโs [3mThe Secret Connexion [0m(Oxford 
University Press, 1989). 

Central to understanding Kant on action and freedom, and on morality generally, is his [3mGroundwork of a Metaphysics of Morals [0m(see, 
for example, the translation by H. J. Paton, Harper & Row, 1964). But Kantโs views are complex and changed over time, even within 
his mature system. One useful discussion is Henry E. Allisonโs [3mKantโs Theory of Freedom [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1990).

There have been countless statements of Compatibilism within the modern English-language tradition. For a short paper, see A. J. 
Ayerโs โFreedom and Necessityโ, in the first edition of the Gary Watson collection on [3mFree Will [0mand in Ayerโs [3mPhilosophical Essays [0m
(New York, 1954); and for a book, Daniel Dennettโs [3mElbow Room [0m(MIT Press, 1984). 

A subtle argument around the place of blame and resentment in human life is Peter Strawsonโs โFreedom and Resentmentโ, in the Gary 
Watson collection. 

Susan Wolf explores the rationalist view that free will and responsibility are to be identified with the capacity to act 
rationally in her [3mFreedom within Reason [0m(Oxford University Press, 1990). 

For the sceptical position see Galen Strawsonโs โThe Impossibility of Moral Responsibilityโ, in the Gary Watson collection, and 
also his [3mFreedom and Belief [0m(Oxford University Press, 1986). 

Harry Frankfurt argues for basing moral responsibility on voluntariness rather than freedom in his โAlternate Possibilities and 
Moral Responsibilityโ, to be found in a collection of his papers [3mThe Importance of What We Care About [0m(Cambridge University Press, 
1988). 

A prominent recent defence of Libertarianism is Robert Kaneโs [3mThe Significance of Free Will [0m(Oxford University Press, 1998). There 
is a good overview of recent libertarian theories in Randolph Clarkeโs [3mLibertarian Accounts of Free Will [0m(Oxford University Press, 
2003). Both these books argue for positions rather different from my own. 

My own views on freedom and action are developed further in my [3mThe Ethics of Action: Action and Self-Determination [0m(Oxford 
University Press, forthcoming). A companion volume [3mThe Ethics of Action: Action and Normativity [0m, will discuss the place of action 
within morality, and in particular the nature of moral obligation.
